<div id="review_743404382" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/743404382" />
    <a title="Zanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna"><img alt="Zanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512813535p2/22591633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/743404382?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zanna" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna">Zanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140449709" href="/book/show/13419553-rash-mon-and-seventeen-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=translated">translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer743404382" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17630612438202122906">First read in 2007<br/><br/>In his characteristically measured, conversational introduction to this book, Murakami Haruki tells us that Akutagawa is his third favourite author in the modern (post 1868) Japanese canon (after Soseki and Tanizaki). Rather than giddily enthusing about the author, Murakami carefully contextualises him in Japanese literature and culture. Akutagawa lived during a brief period of prosperity and political liberalism between WWI and the Depression in 1929, and combined appreciative</span>
  <span id="freeText17630612438202122906" style="display:none">First read in 2007<br /><br />In his characteristically measured, conversational introduction to this book, Murakami Haruki tells us that Akutagawa is his third favourite author in the modern (post 1868) Japanese canon (after Soseki and Tanizaki). Rather than giddily enthusing about the author, Murakami carefully contextualises him in Japanese literature and culture. Akutagawa lived during a brief period of prosperity and political liberalism between WWI and the Depression in 1929, and combined appreciative immersion in Japanese cultural life with passion for Western literature. He called Soseki 'The Master', and worked as an editor as well as writing; the autobiographical stories in this collection demonstrate the literariness of his short life.<br /><br />As well as drawing on his own daily experience and mental anguish for source material (a form related to the 'I-novel' style of some of his contemporaries in Japanese literature), Akutagawa wrote stories set in the Edo period when Japan was governed by military overlords. Many of these have interesting historical content. He also wrote stories of his own time, with an amusing self-awareness. The writer is always a presence, even if only in the pleasurable excess of musical names.<br /><br />His style is clear, lyrical and pierced here and there by vivid images, like the purple sparks made by the tram on the overhead wires that the narrator wants to hold in his hands. Murakami says 'the flow of his language... moves along like a living thing', and this translation by Jay Rubin certainly preserves this natural, dynamic, unforced quality. <br /><br />One technique that I particularly liked and that is original or at least unusual is the one he employs in 'Hell Screen' as well as other pieces, that of using a disapproving narrator to tell the hero's story. This device not only actively engages the reader's sympathy with the protagonists, but also creates a deep and nuanced impression of social exclusion and isolation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17630612438202122906" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_131865893" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/131865893" />
    <a title="L.S. Popovich" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4567704-l-s-popovich"><img alt="L.S. Popovich" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537455199p2/4567704.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/131865893?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="L.S. Popovich" class="user" itemprop="url" name="L.S. Popovich" href="/user/show/4567704-l-s-popovich">L.S. Popovich</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4567704-l-s-popovich?shelf=all-time-favorite">all-time-favorite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4567704-l-s-popovich?shelf=japanese-obsession">japanese-obsession</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4567704-l-s-popovich?shelf=best-short-story-collections-ever">best-short-story-collections-ever</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4567704-l-s-popovich?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4567704-l-s-popovich?shelf=short-story-colection">short-story-colection</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer131865893" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17404732721976175552">Akutagawa is one of my favorite writers. He took his own life with barbiturates at age 35 and left behind some 300 stories, sketches, articles and literary experiments. In English he has appeared in over a dozen collections of the same 20-30 most famous stories retranslated a dozen times. This latest collection, translated by the consummate Jay Rubin, has a lovingly detailed introduction by the inimitable Haruki Murakami. It is a mere sampling of 18 stories from his impossibly good body of work.</span>
  <span id="freeText17404732721976175552" style="display:none">Akutagawa is one of my favorite writers. He took his own life with barbiturates at age 35 and left behind some 300 stories, sketches, articles and literary experiments. In English he has appeared in over a dozen collections of the same 20-30 most famous stories retranslated a dozen times. This latest collection, translated by the consummate Jay Rubin, has a lovingly detailed introduction by the inimitable Haruki Murakami. It is a mere sampling of 18 stories from his impossibly good body of work. Unlike Toson, Soseki and Tanizaki, Akutagawa did not embark on massive literary projects. Instead, he honed his craft with precision and an appreciation for classic storytelling. I have read some of his stories ten times, and they always elicit a strong response from me. <br /><br />In a lot of ways, he resembles Gogol, and even composed an homage with his story "The Nose." Though different in content, the tone is reminiscent of the Russian master. This is one of the masterpieces contained in this treasury. The others include: "Rashomon, In a Bamboo Grove, Hell Screen, Spinning Gears, Death Register, and the Life of a Stupid Man." Even the ones that are not masterworks per se, are extremely entertaining. "Green Onions, The Story of the Head that Fell Off, Horse Legs, and Loyalty" fall into this category. If you are new to this author, you may not enjoy all of his tales, but I believe you will appreciate many aspects of his singular talent.<br /><br />He writes a few different types of stories: 1). retellings of classic tales from Chinese and other sources. These read a little like fables. 2.) Autobiographical tales: these are often depressing, taking details of his haunted life and casting them bleakly against the backdrop of his times. 3). Religious tales like "Christ of Nanking" (not included in the collection) and others. Historical tales, taking place well before the author's time but possessing uncanny verisimilitude.<br /><br />In his stories you will find traces of his influences: Anatole France, Strindberg, Merimee, Goethe, Nagoya Shiga, Soseki, Toson, Tanizaki, Basho, Doppo, Ogai, Pu Songling and dozens of other European and Chinese authors. He has rewritten stories from Pu Songling's collections as well as retold many from the seminal Japanese proto-mythologies.<br /><br />Akutagawa draws from Buddhism, Shintoism, Christianity and Myth. I think he is one of the most interesting writers I have ever encountered because he processes other literary worlds into new forms. Even when he waxes esoteric, he is charming and insightful. He explores human nature with deep characters and memorable comedy and tragedy.<br /><br />This brilliant edition includes thorough notes by Rubin explaining the finer points of the stories. There is enough material in this singular Penguin edition to write a dissertation on Akutagawa. Jay Rubin has put in an astounding effort toward accuracy and illumination. I only wish he would continue with further volumes of stories.<br /><br />If you appreciate the stories of Chekhov, Gogol, Maupassant, and Dostoyevsky, you will find a lot to love about this author. Typically, you can expect tortured artists, explorations of morality and death, futility and hope, love and loss. Very classic themes. "Green Onions" and "O-gin" were odd but welcome selections for this book. Overall, it is the most well-rounded collection of the author's writings in English.<br /><br />I have so far discovered 107 Akutagawa tales in English. I've read every anthology of Japanese literature, every collection of his tales and tracked down out of print Japanese-American periodicals through JSTOR. I want to thank Ryan C. K. Choi and N. A. Feathers for publishing new translations of his work on their websites. This incredible author has not gotten a full treatment in English and I implore translators to get to work on making his complete works available. So far we have only about 900 pages of stories, when obscure, ancient masters like Pu Songling have been translated more comprehensively. Along with this collection you will want to read two more collections: Mandarins, translated by Charles de Wolf, and The Beautiful and Grotesque, which includes "Kappa," his novella.<br /><br />Though Akutagawa's accomplishment is profoundly important (far more so, I would argue, than Murakami claims in his indicting introduction), one wonders what heights Akutagawa might have reached had he endured the agonies of his intellectual rigors for decades longer. Was he capable of writing novels? Were the demons he wrote about in "Spinning Gears" exaggerated or as sincerely recorded as in Strindberg's Inferno? These questions will never be answered. But part of his appeal is how digestible and varied his work is.<br /><br />This is undoubtedly one of the greatest short story collections by any Japanese or non-Western author.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17404732721976175552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1954147881" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1954147881" />
    <a title="martina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38218534-martina"><img alt="martina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546272270p2/38218534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1954147881?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="martina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="martina" href="/user/show/38218534-martina">martina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140449709" href="/book/show/13419553-rash-mon-and-seventeen-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38218534-martina?shelf=pure-art-of-words">pure-art-of-words</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38218534-martina?shelf=classics-checklist">classics-checklist</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1954147881" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17250628376924710814">Throughout my life I've been experiencing the strangest tendency when reading a really great literary work: after finishing a particularly brilliant passage/story/poem, I just have to put the book down for while, to stop reading it altogether as if I was afraid that this was the peak and nothing better will follow. Sometimes this takes days of sweet pondering upon the writer's craft. I like savouring these moments, they occur rarely, bringing me much pleasure and gently nudging me into thinking </span>
  <span id="freeText17250628376924710814" style="display:none">Throughout my life I've been experiencing the strangest tendency when reading a really great literary work: after finishing a particularly brilliant passage/story/poem, I just have to put the book down for while, to stop reading it altogether as if I was afraid that this was the peak and nothing better will follow. Sometimes this takes days of sweet pondering upon the writer's craft. I like savouring these moments, they occur rarely, bringing me much pleasure and gently nudging me into thinking about beauty. Many Akutagawa's short stories gave me this feeling, especially those that were about medieval Japan and the samurai (especially <i>In a Bamboo Grove</i>, <i>Hell Screen</i> or <i>Loyalty</i>). I find it extremely hard to comment on such brilliantly crafted small literary worlds. It's always difficult to describe perfection, you just have to experience it and then you know: this is <i>it</i>. I feel the compliments are also in order for Jay Rubin, the translator, who rendered these stories so beautifully into English.<br /><br />On the other hand, this book seemed utterly depressing to me. Akutagawa is preoccupied with death and suicide in quite an unhealthy manner, which is surely due to his mental illness. Naturally, this issue is a fact of life, not a bad thing in itself, but the way this volume is compiled and filled with incredibly elaborate commentary on the autobiographical element of his writing, one sort of feels like sinking into that utter darkness with the author (the most dismal posthumously published stories are ordered in the end of this compilation). Thus, Akutagawa would be one of those artists that I put into a special category of mine - the writers that I consider truly gifted (if not even genius), but for various reasons I can't relate to them personally. <i>His own works were unlikely to appeal to people who were not like him and had not lived a life like this</i> ...as Akutagawa wrote himself. He tells us later that he is the one that believes in the existence of darkness only, not in the light. It leaves me just wondering how much of this is the result his depression or unhappy personal circumstances and how much the influence of fin-de-siècle literature he was devouring, an influence on his life that is duly acknowledged. <br /><br />So, there he is - a master of words, a strange man... I guess this is the black and the white of this reading experience. A damn intense reading experience.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17250628376924710814" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_211588221" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/211588221" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/211588221?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer211588221" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12455051912343612802">"Rashomon" tells the story of a "lowly servant" sheltering from the rain on the steps of a rashomon (outer castle gate). He has recently been laid off and sits pondering his future. He hears a sound and ventures inside the rashomon to see what it was. Inside are heaps of dead bodies from the recent plague and a strange old woman wandering about, going through the corpses' clothes. The servant attacks the old woman, strips her of her clothing, throws her onto the heap, and runs off. <br/><br/>"In a Bamboo </span>
  <span id="freeText12455051912343612802" style="display:none">"Rashomon" tells the story of a "lowly servant" sheltering from the rain on the steps of a rashomon (outer castle gate). He has recently been laid off and sits pondering his future. He hears a sound and ventures inside the rashomon to see what it was. Inside are heaps of dead bodies from the recent plague and a strange old woman wandering about, going through the corpses' clothes. The servant attacks the old woman, strips her of her clothing, throws her onto the heap, and runs off. <br /><br />"In a Bamboo Grove" features a married couple and a robber. The story is told from the perspective of all witnesses and it emerges that the husband was murdered but who did it and why is the mystery. <br /><br />These are the two most famous Akutagawa stories and are an excellent start to the collection. However, afterwards they become quite mediocre and even a bit tedious. The forced gothic of "Hell Screen" plods along until a near hysterical ending that undermines the seriousness of the story, that of obssession and the artistic mind. "The Nose" is a very odd story about a priest with a very big nose, has it shortened, and it grows back again. It's one of those "be grateful for what you have, accept who you are" type tales and not nearly as brilliant as Gogol's "The Nose" (Gogol being one of Akutagawa's influences and, frankly, a better short story writer). <br /><br />As the title suggests there are 18 stories here but those are the only ones I can remember. The last couple in the section called "Akutagawa's Own Story" are interesting, with "Life of a Stupid Man" playing with form and presenting an interesting take on autobiography through small snippets of a life glimpsed in passing. "Spinning Gears" is the final story he wrote before his suicide (pills) and is about the slowly disintegrating mind of Akutagawa. The desperation and mounting paranoia give the reader an insight into Akutagawa's fragile and fractured mindset. The strange imagery is also fascinating. The spinning gears he sees around his eyes confuse and scare him while at every turn he sees signs of death - a decaying animal corpse, dying people in hospitals, and above all his morbid fear of going insane like his mother. <br /><br />I won't say I didn't enjoy the book as there were some stories here that were excellent, and whether it's Jay Rubin's translation or not, the writing was always of a high standard. And students of literature will find reading "Rashomon" and "In a Bamboo Grove" very rewarding as will film students who are interested in the work of Kurasawa who based his film "Rashomon" on those stories. But compared to other short story writers and other Japanese writers, Akutagawa isn't nearly on their level. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12455051912343612802" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_324010743" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/324010743" />
    <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes"><img alt="aPriL does feral sometimes " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478243665p2/3409532.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/324010743?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="user" itemprop="url" name="aPriL does feral sometimes " href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes">aPriL does feral sometimes </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140449709" href="/book/show/13419553-rash-mon-and-seventeen-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=literary">literary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer324010743" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1677798025323432052">The stories included in this collection reflect an author who is intelligent, well-read, perceptive and deeply aware of human foibles. Half of the short stories are very entertaining and revealing, written in the cadences of ancient Eastern folk tales. <br/><br/>The author wrote these stories in the book in the years 1915 to 1925, in Japan, using Chinese and Japanese literary and cultural themes. But they not only educate the reader in the themes of Eastern literature, they also demonstrate that humanity </span>
  <span id="freeText1677798025323432052" style="display:none">The stories included in this collection reflect an author who is intelligent, well-read, perceptive and deeply aware of human foibles. Half of the short stories are very entertaining and revealing, written in the cadences of ancient Eastern folk tales. <br /><br />The author wrote these stories in the book in the years 1915 to 1925, in Japan, using Chinese and Japanese literary and cultural themes. But they not only educate the reader in the themes of Eastern literature, they also demonstrate that humanity is the same whether living in the East or West, no matter what century. The way people feel about the mysteries of their lives is the same whatever the setting. At the same time, the different, distinctly Eastern cultural prism through which our commonalities present themselves reflect a Japanese view in these stories that is a bit mystifying, and very interesting. <br /><br />This collection includes two very famous stories, ‘Rashomon’ and ‘In a Bamboo Grove’, which were combined together into the plot of a famous Japanese movie, which in turn Hollywood has not so aptly attempted to imitate in several variations. A character’s character in point of view is certainly shown to be important in deciding veracity of a tale! <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[For the record, in the story Rashomon I believe the ghost, Tekehiro, is the most truthful. I believe he incorrectly reads his wife's actions and motivations, but he was the most truthful as he understood what happened.<br /><br />Tekehiro’s wife, Masago, saw in Tekehiro what she interpreted as contempt in his face after she was raped. I believe she misread her husband's emotion. She saw what she feared most. She continuously lost consciousness and was extremely stressed. She had been raped, after all. Tajomaru, the rapist, was also a braggart and a criminal. He probably did not feel he would be let go or considered innocent after his capture by authorities. He would be most interested in inflating his actions, so I don't believe him in that there was a fight between him and Tekehiro. <br /><br />After raping her, Tajomaru, I think, added insult to injury and tormented Masago, only increasing her distress and her certainty that her husband would despise her. In reality (?!?), the thief only wanted to rape, torment and rob the couple. Masago would have been wild with shame and fear of rejection by her husband, as well as feeling hopeless in having been degraded. <br /><br />Tekehiro would have been dishonored and shamed by his impotence, greed, dupability and perhaps his suspicion that his wife lost respect for him. This would lead him to believe she could easily want him dead for that, and thus he would be blind to how she might be hiding her own shameful acts of wanting to save her life and honor by going with the thief, whom she believed wanted to take her with him (I think that's what he whispered to her in the short story). <br /><br />The thief forced her to publicize her choice of living over loyalty, so, by his trick, she shamed herself twice over by her choice of deserting her husband. When the thief revealed his trick, she was triply humiliated - first the rape, and then her choice of deserting her husband, then in being fooled and rejected. After the thief left (why would he untie the husband, realistically?) I think, unable to face her wretched self-betrayal and afraid of her husband's supposed need for revenge and rage, she did try to stab him, but perhaps incompetently. She was 19, a young girl. After she ran away, her husband killed himself in self-loathing. The girl's mom said he was kind at heart, and he was a man of honor. At least that's how it makes sense to me, given what I know of human nature.<br /><br />Of course, uncertain narrators abound, so what all of us judges can go by is what we know of human nature along with the testimony. Akutagawa has made the reader one of the characters in the story, has he not? As judges, truth must be assigned as a probability instead of a certainty. Although I believe in my above synopsis as the closest truth, Tajomaru confessed. I believe he raped and robbed and played a horrible spiteful trick on the couple which doomed the marriage, but as a judge, I don't believe he dueled or killed the husband physically. Tajomaru committed soul murders on the couple, perhaps. But Tajomuro has confessed out of misplaced pride and braggedo, and I'm happy to condemn him for the murder, given no other physical evidence.<br /><br />The author is so clever. Now WE know the uncertainty of determining truth of phenomena which is dependent on human interpretations of reality. I have become an unreliable narrator.<br /><br />I completely disbelieve the Woodcutter. Despite appearances of being disinterested, his story does not make any sense unless you accept the complete dissonance of a young wife being more like a hardened psychopathic prostitute moments after being abducted, imprisoned, and raped, by an armed man threatening death and humiliation.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Later stories included in the book, from 1925 to 1927 are more autobiographical or personal, and while revealing how similar that people feel about fear, stress, anxiety, and how we live within communal society, sadly, these somewhat autobiographical stories show how the author is losing his equilibrium and peace of mind. It is difficult to parse out if it is the fear of going insane or if it is actual instability precipitating the author’s emotional frailty.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1677798025323432052" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_114512075" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/114512075" />
    <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly"><img alt="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349701607p2/2190636.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/114512075?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8231376-rashomon-and-seventeen-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer114512075" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13179596212727258094">When I read my first Murakami, a compilation of short stories called "After the Quake," I was amazed by his refreshing originality. Some of his stories, indeed, had the effect of an earthquake to me. There were jolting, sudden and unexpected turns. In one, a man and a woman, after a brief introduction, make love. Then, out of nowhere, the man felt a sudden impulse to kill her. In another story, the characters were on a beach. Tears suddenly flow down from the eyes of one character, then they tal</span>
  <span id="freeText13179596212727258094" style="display:none">When I read my first Murakami, a compilation of short stories called "After the Quake," I was amazed by his refreshing originality. Some of his stories, indeed, had the effect of an earthquake to me. There were jolting, sudden and unexpected turns. In one, a man and a woman, after a brief introduction, make love. Then, out of nowhere, the man felt a sudden impulse to kill her. In another story, the characters were on a beach. Tears suddenly flow down from the eyes of one character, then they talk of killing themselves. Developments like these come without warning as they were not even hinted in the previous narration.<br /><br />Now, it can be told. This style of storytelling is not at all original. Murakami, I think, copied it from this great Japanese writer who killed himself in 1927 at the age of 35--Ryunosuke Akutagawa (in the list of the 1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die he's under letter "R"--a mistake, because his surname is Akutagawa, not Ryunosuke). By his own confession, Murakami has been reading Akutagawa since he was a teenager.<br /><br />I bet that if one includes Rashomon in that compilation "After the Quake" an uninitiated reader will not find any difference between it and the rest of the stories there except that the latter have modern settings while "Rashomon" is set in 12th century Japan.<br /><br />"Rashomon" ("Mon" being the Japanese for gate) was an outer castle gate, specifically the great southern main entrance to Kyoto during the Heian Period. It had massive pillars, towering archways and several chambers. The whole city of Kyoto, at the time of the story, was under civil unrest. People were hungry, even the Rashomon itself had become dilapidated for lack of proper maintenance. In the upper chamber thereof was dumped the city's dead, the victims of the prevailing hunger and violence.<br /><br />In the lower chamber sits a servant who had just lost his job. He is said to be waiting for the rain to stop but he knows that even if the rain stops he really has nowhere to go and nothing to do. He had come to the dreadful conclusion that he would either die of starvation or survive by being a thief.<br /><br />The mood is somber, the descriptive prose is elegant, fluid and spontaneous. Just like Murakami (in his short stories). Then the servant sees something...Thereafter comes one event after another which felt like Murakami's earthquakes, with a similar ending that leaves unanswered questions.<br /><br />In the Introduction to the life and works of Akutagawa a Japanese literary critic, in 1917, described him (Akutagawa) as "a writer who can't write without props." I was amused by this because elsewhere in goodreads before, discussing Kafka on the Shore with a Murakami fanatic K.D., I wrote that Murakami had used a lot of juvenile, ineffective props here (like talking cats, mother and son fucking, eels falling down from the sky, etc.) which were not even original as you can see parallels from Greek mythology and even in works of Lewis Carroll. <br /><br />The Introduction also declared:<br /><br />"Sheer technique...though skillfully applied, does not necessarily translate into original literature. A fictional world that was not truly (the author's) own and that used borrowed containers would eventually reach and impasse and come to stand in his way like a high wall. Further pursuit of fictional method could only yield technical polish. And not surprisingly, the novelty would wear thin and readers would tire of seeing the same devices."<br /><br />Wow, I myself could have said this after reading Kafka on the Shore! Now, guess who wrote this introduction? Close your eyes and bang your head on the wall--Haruki Murakami himself.<br /><br />LOL!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13179596212727258094" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1328823312" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1328823312" />
    <a title="Madhulika Liddle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16294266-madhulika-liddle"><img alt="Madhulika Liddle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517826893p2/16294266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1328823312?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Madhulika Liddle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Madhulika Liddle" href="/user/show/16294266-madhulika-liddle">Madhulika Liddle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140449709" href="/book/show/13419553-rash-mon-and-seventeen-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1328823312" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16826794664580947258">It’s hard to review something like Ryūnosuke Akutagawa’s <i> Rashōmon and Seventeen Other Stories</i>: it’s too complex, too often inducing a <i>“What was that I read?”</i>, too deep, and yet approachable, very readable. <br/><br/>I found this book by chance while surfing Goodreads, and was immediately attracted by <i> Rashōmon</i>, since I am a fan of Kurosawa’s, and am all admiration for that particular film (the plot of which, ironically, draws more from Akutagawa’s <i>In the Bamboo Grove</i> than it does from <i> Rashōmon</i> itself). </span>
  <span id="freeText16826794664580947258" style="display:none">It’s hard to review something like Ryūnosuke Akutagawa’s <i> Rashōmon and Seventeen Other Stories</i>: it’s too complex, too often inducing a <i>“What was that I read?”</i>, too deep, and yet approachable, very readable. <br /><br />I found this book by chance while surfing Goodreads, and was immediately attracted by <i> Rashōmon</i>, since I am a fan of Kurosawa’s, and am all admiration for that particular film (the plot of which, ironically, draws more from Akutagawa’s <i>In the Bamboo Grove</i> than it does from <i> Rashōmon</i> itself). I bought the book and told myself that I’d read it slowly, one short story at a time—and found myself caught like a fly in a spider’s web. I ended up reading most of this book in long stretches, until my eyes hurt. <br /><br />The eighteen stories are very diverse in nature: there are ghosts here, and dragons. There is hell, both somewhat distant, seen through the benevolent eyes of the Sakyamuni in <i>The Spider Thread</i>, <i>and</i> close, horrifying, macabre—as in <i>Hell Screen</i>. There are tales of a Japan torn between tradition and modernity, of battles between Christianity and older, local beliefs. There are battles, too, between man and man, and—most forcibly, most searingly, the stories of man’s battles with himself. The latter are brought most vividly (and disturbingly) to life in the last section of the book, which brings together six autobiographical stories by Akutagawa. <br /><br />As I progressed through this book, I went through a range of emotions. I laughed at the quirky humour in <i>The Nose, Dragon: The Old Potter’s Tale</i> and <i>Green Onions</i>. I shivered at the cruelty of <i>Hell Screen</i>. And I couldn’t help but wonder how much of <i>Spinning Gears</i> (which was published posthumously, after Akutagawa killed himself in 1927) was real, and how much was not. <br /><br />This is a brilliant book. It’s rich, textured, detailed. The imagery is often breathtaking (“<i>She had a radiant face, like the morning sun on a thin sheet of ice”</i>), and the layers are fascinating, peeling away from the mundane, even comical, to the wryly profound. As an example: <i>”He put a cigarette in his mouth and was striking a match when he collapsed face-down on his desk and died. It was a truly disappointing way to die. Fortunately, however, society rarely offers critical comment regarding the way a person dies. The way a person lives is what evokes criticism</i>.”<br /><br />The translator, Jay Rubin, also provides useful notes on stories and their elements, which help in a better understanding of the story (especially for someone not familiar with Japan and its culture). In addition, there’s a brief but good biography of Akutagawa, and a superb introduction by Haruki Murakami. I would advise reading all of these, besides the stories that comprise the book: they help get a better insight into the author, and so add to the experience. <br /><br />I ended this book feeling both oddly deflated <i>and</i> inspired. As a writer, I can’t help but be inspired by writing of such stature. As a writer, too, I can’t help but feel that I cannot possibly ever write as brilliantly as this. Not a book I am going to forget in a hurry, if ever. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16826794664580947258" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_689183099" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/689183099" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2242482-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1326923603p2/2242482.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/689183099?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/2242482-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140449709" href="/book/show/13419553-rash-mon-and-seventeen-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2242482-david?shelf=big-red-circle">big-red-circle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer689183099" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10833663004817081677">In his (undated ... perhaps 2006?) introduction, Haruki Murakami gives us what he thinks would be Japan's 10 most important "writers of national stature". They are writers that "left us works of the first rank that vividly reflect the mentality of the Japanese people ... [the works] must have the power to survive at least a quarter century after the writer's death. ... The important thing is whether each of them as an individual human being embraced an awareness of the great questions of the age</span>
  <span id="freeText10833663004817081677" style="display:none">In his (undated ... perhaps 2006?) introduction, Haruki Murakami gives us what he thinks would be Japan's 10 most important "writers of national stature". They are writers that "left us works of the first rank that vividly reflect the mentality of the Japanese people ... [the works] must have the power to survive at least a quarter century after the writer's death. ... The important thing is whether each of them as an individual human being embraced an awareness of the great questions of the age, accepted his or her social responsibility as an artist on the front line, and made an honest effort to shape his or her life accordingly."<br /><br />Haruki believes the top 10 to be:<br />#1. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/35235.Natsume_S_seki" title="Natsume Sōseki" rel="nofollow noopener">Sōseki Natsume</a>. Equal #2 are: 2.<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6894100.Akutagawa_Ry_nosuke" title="Akutagawa Ryūnosuke" rel="nofollow noopener">Akutagawa Ryūnosuke</a>, 2.<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/82910._gai_Mori" title="Ōgai Mori" rel="nofollow noopener">Ōgai Mori</a>, 4.<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Shimazaki%20To%20son" title="Shimazaki To son" rel="nofollow noopener">Shimazaki To son</a>, 5.<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6894097.Shiga_Naoya" title="Shiga Naoya" rel="nofollow noopener">Shiga Naoya</a>, 6.<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6263.Jun_ichir__Tanizaki" title="Jun'ichirō Tanizaki" rel="nofollow noopener">Jun'ichirō Tanizaki</a>, 7.<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8550.Yasunari_Kawabata" title="Yasunari Kawabata" rel="nofollow noopener">Yasunari Kawabata</a>. Equal #8 are: 8.<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/113561.Osamu_Dazai" title="Osamu Dazai" rel="nofollow noopener">Osamu Dazai</a>, 8.<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/35258.Yukio_Mishima" title="Yukio Mishima" rel="nofollow noopener">Yukio Mishima</a>.<br /><br />He can't think of a tenth name for the list(!). Which is cute if you are a fan of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3439713.Kenzabur___e" title="Kenzaburō Ōe" rel="nofollow noopener">Kenzaburō Ōe</a>. Haruki is such a bitch. He goes on to say that "Kawabata's works, to be honest, have always been a problem for me. ... I have never been able to identify very closely with his fictional world." And "With reagrd to Shimazaki and Shiga, I can only say that I have no particular interest in them ... what I have read has left little trace in my memory."<br /><br />So old Kenzaburo has been excluded from Haruki's list of 10 ... which only includes 9 ... and of which, 3 he really doesn't have time for? Ouch.<br /><br />But Haruki's obviously mad. He seems to say that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/169437.Botchan" title="Botchan by Natsume Sōseki" rel="nofollow noopener">Botchan</a> can be "memorized whole by most school children". What?<br /><br />On to Akutagawa ... I liked the "I" stuff the best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10833663004817081677" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2098365921" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2098365921" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2098365921?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2098365921" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11450013808078107086">Verdant vignettes vibrate across the reader’s eyes, as the are drawn into the splendiferous similes which dance across the page, shimmering like the pale reflection of sun-light on pebbles in a Japanese garden. Akutagawa fused he aesthetics of haiku with the psychology of Dostoevsky and other Western writers; style and form are as central to his stories as structure, psychology and characters, yet few short story writers are able to match the sheer diversity of Akutagawa’s ouvre; whether it be G</span>
  <span id="freeText11450013808078107086" style="display:none">Verdant vignettes vibrate across the reader’s eyes, as the are drawn into the splendiferous similes which dance across the page, shimmering like the pale reflection of sun-light on pebbles in a Japanese garden. Akutagawa fused he aesthetics of haiku with the psychology of Dostoevsky and other Western writers; style and form are as central to his stories as structure, psychology and characters, yet few short story writers are able to match the sheer diversity of Akutagawa’s ouvre; whether it be Gogolian fantasy in ‘The Nose’, discourses on the power of art in ‘Hell Screen’ or proto magical realism in ‘Horse Legs’, the breadth of Akutagawa’s stories in immense.<br /><br />The highlight of these stories is undoubtedly ‘Hell Screen’, a macabre exploration of the darker elements of artistic inspiration, as the titular character, the morose and morbid painter Yoshide, tortures others to gain inspiration for his depiction of hell. Other highlights include, ‘In a Budding Grove’ which explores the nature of narrative perspective and was the inspiration for Kurosawa’s ‘Rashomon’ and ‘Spinning Gears’ a playful and poetic l pieces which reminders the reader of the jejunish experimental fiction of Oulipo. <br /><br />Interspersed within the short stories are some strikingly pretty images, such as;<br /><br />“Pine boughs stretched across the empty sky, and in them hung a copper-coloured moon devoid or radiance.”<br /><br />“A butterfly fluttered it’s wings in a wind thick with the smell of seaweed. His dry lips felt the touch of the butterfly for the briefest instant, yet the wisp of a wing dust still shone on his lips years later.”<br /><br />“I left the hotel and hurried towards my sister’s house along streets reflecting blue skys in pools of snow-melt”<br /><br />Loneliness and alienation are the central themes which run through these stories, as the characters struggle to articulate their euphoria-whether it be religious in the exploration of incipient Christianity in ‘O-Gin’ (“O-Gin’s heart was not, like her parents, a desert swept by searing winds. It was an abundant field of ripened wheat sprinkled with wild roses”) or of the aspiring artist, Yasukichi, whose cloyingly clichéd funeral orations are held in higher regards than his art. More than this, his stories are about love and disillusions and jjoys there is something quintessentially modern the urban romance in ‘Green Onions’. Akutagawa is, along with Chekhov and Katherine Mansfield, one of the greatest short story writers. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11450013808078107086" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1506847958" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1506847958" />
    <a title="David J" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6494653-david-j"><img alt="David J" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471823744p2/6494653.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1506847958?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David J" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David J" href="/user/show/6494653-david-j">David J</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6494653-david-j?shelf=purchased-2016">purchased-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6494653-david-j?shelf=in-translation">in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6494653-david-j?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6494653-david-j?shelf=japanese">japanese</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6494653-david-j?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1506847958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6044800890379083640">I first came by Ryūnosuke Akutagawa via Akira Kurosawa’s 1950 film <i>Rashōmon</i>. It’s an adaptation of two short stories within this collection: “In a Bamboo Grove” and “Rashōmon” (though Kurosawa adapted the former and simply took the name of the latter and used it as a framing device). It’s one of my favorite Kurosawa films, and so I knew I’d like to check out the source material at some point. I’m glad I did, because there are some really good stories here and it’s an overall well-rounded antholo</span>
  <span id="freeText6044800890379083640" style="display:none">I first came by Ryūnosuke Akutagawa via Akira Kurosawa’s 1950 film <i>Rashōmon</i>. It’s an adaptation of two short stories within this collection: “In a Bamboo Grove” and “Rashōmon” (though Kurosawa adapted the former and simply took the name of the latter and used it as a framing device). It’s one of my favorite Kurosawa films, and so I knew I’d like to check out the source material at some point. I’m glad I did, because there are some really good stories here and it’s an overall well-rounded anthology.<br /><br />This collection is divided into four sections: A World in Decay, Under the Sword, Modern Tragicomedy, and Akutagawa's Own Story. Akutagawa crosses genres throughout these stories--from comedy to drama to autobiography to Jidaigeki to the Gothic--and a lot of them work out quite well. I’m a particular fan of “In a Bamboo Grove,” “Hell Screen,” “The Nose,” “The Spider Thread,” and “Green Onions.” The autobiographical stories in the last section, while at times quite fascinating in learning about Akutagawa’s inner turmoil, don’t read as well as the previous stories; they’re interesting and informational, but they’re ultimately a little hard to get into and not as entertaining as the previous stories.<br /><br />Akutagawa is a great writer. He oftentimes has some slick and inviting prose, which makes for easy and enjoyable reading. His well-placed comedic touches were surprising too. However, if you aren’t that educated on Japanese history and geography, like me, then you may struggle a bit with the references. There are extremely helpful note indications throughout, but that means flipping to the back of the book, which does disrupt the flow of reading. I’d definitely recommend this collection, but some might want to proceed with caution.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6044800890379083640" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7366675-morgan?shelf=translation">translation</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1759906604" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13615674163572387230"><i>"It's unfortunate for the gods that, unlike us, they cannot commit suicide."</i><br/><br/><i>"I don't have the strength to keep writing this. To go on living with this feeling is painful beyond description. Isn't there someone kind enough to strangle me in my sleep?"</i><br/><br/>Two quote that stood out to me and I only can understand them after looking up about this writer. He seemed to have a lot going on in his mind unfortunately, but he seemed to write a lot short stories before he killed himself. I guess when reading th</span>
  <span id="freeText13615674163572387230" style="display:none"><i>"It's unfortunate for the gods that, unlike us, they cannot commit suicide."</i><br /><br /><i>"I don't have the strength to keep writing this. To go on living with this feeling is painful beyond description. Isn't there someone kind enough to strangle me in my sleep?"</i><br /><br />Two quote that stood out to me and I only can understand them after looking up about this writer. He seemed to have a lot going on in his mind unfortunately, but he seemed to write a lot short stories before he killed himself. I guess when reading these stories, keep in mind the author's fate.<br /><br />For me, these were oddly written. The style wasn't really what I was use too. I think the three stories that stood out to me were "The Nose," "Hell Screen," and "The Life of a Stupid Man." Usually I like the title story, but not really this time around.<br /><br />However, I'm not really sure I fully liked Ryūnosuke Akutagawa work. He does remind me if Kafka and H.C. Anderson wrote a book together. "Hell Screen" was like Kafka and "The Nose" was like Anderson. He stories had this dream-like and fairy-tale-like feel to them at times.<br /><br />Some of these stories are worth reading, but I have a feeling most people I know won't really care for these. Not sure who'd I recommend these too unless you are interested in Japanese short story fiction.</span>

<div id="review_1735741963" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1735741963" />
    <a title="Erin the Avid Reader ⚜BFF&#39;s with the Cheshire Cat⚜" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat"><img alt="Erin the Avid Reader ⚜BFF&#39;s with the Cheshire Cat⚜" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397575578p2/23003918.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1735741963?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin the Avid Reader ⚜BFF&#39;s with the Cheshire Cat⚜" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin the Avid Reader ⚜BFF&#39;s with the Cheshire Cat⚜" href="/user/show/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat">Erin the Avid Reader ⚜BFF&#39;s with the Cheshire Cat⚜</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=is-a-movie">is-a-movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=the-human-brain-and-body">the-human-brain-and-body</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=reading-the-world">reading-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=my-book-reviews">my-book-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=books-that-make-you-think">books-that-make-you-think</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=powells-25-books-your-should-read">powells-25-books-your-should-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1735741963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15052141899478601172">Wow...just...wow.<br/><br/>I never though I could find myself this immersed in a book before and finish it this quickly. The last time I finished a long book this quickly was 4-5 years ago when I read Jonathan Stroud's "The Amulet of Samarkand" in one night. This was a good book to start reading the night of my birthday. What a real treat indeed!<br/><br/>I was expecting to finish this AFTER "A Man of All Seasons", which I was already over halfway done with and I got there from only two days worth of reading...but </span>
  <span id="freeText15052141899478601172" style="display:none">Wow...just...wow.<br /><br />I never though I could find myself this immersed in a book before and finish it this quickly. The last time I finished a long book this quickly was 4-5 years ago when I read Jonathan Stroud's "The Amulet of Samarkand" in one night. This was a good book to start reading the night of my birthday. What a real treat indeed!<br /><br />I was expecting to finish this AFTER "A Man of All Seasons", which I was already over halfway done with and I got there from only two days worth of reading...but nope. This beat it to the finish line. It was that addicting...almost as addicting as sugar it was!<br /><br />My mom picked up this book from Powell's after seeing the movie. Not hearing of this author before, I picked it up because of its errors cover and was hooked! I finished the 236 (268 if you count the afterwards and the notes) page behemoth of short stories and a short novella.<br /><br />There is something really creepy and superb in the way Ryonosuke Akutagawa writes his stories. He is really good at describing what he's trying to convey. However, the final three stories (which were both autobiographical and semi-autobiographical) of this collection were a bit tricky for me to read sue to the suicidal undertones of each and every one of them (Ryonosuke commit suicide when he was 35, and at the end of his last two stories he left notes stating that there was no point in him living anymore. Creepy and lachrymose all in one package)! The autobiographical ones are mostly about him just observing why he's on Earth and wonders what his purpose is. I have never ever read an author who put suicide notes in their stories but there's gotta be a first time (it's sad as hell so be warned).<br /><br />Ryonosuke did stories that included horror, cynicism, comedy, drama, realism, and of course, beauty. Every single one of his stories is beautifully written and you can tell that he was passionate about what he was doing (excluding the later ones). He was definitely a genius and of course deserves having a Japanese literary award named in his honor. This man really deserves it.<br /><br />The best story in here was probably "The Bamboo Grove", which tells the story of a murder from the perspective of many people...including the spirit of the murdered! A very, very compelling and short story indeed.<br /><br />If he had not killed himself, I wonder what other work he would have produced. For such a short writing career, he was indeed a prolific writer.<br /><br />In his memory, please pick up this book and at least give it a try. This man is a genius and needs more love for what he created.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15052141899478601172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Smiley " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2580971-smiley"><img alt="Smiley " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525349534p2/2580971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/96761452?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Smiley " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Smiley " href="/user/show/2580971-smiley">Smiley </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2580971-smiley?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2580971-smiley?shelf=japan">japan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer96761452" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15309435841169151935">Reading Ryunosuke Akutakawa's "Hell Screen" is like reading Edgar Allan Poe. However, "Rashomon" here was merely the inception of the 1950 film directed by Akira Kurosawa since, according to the Translator's Note, the director used only the first two short stories (Rashomon & In a Bamboo Grove) and Shinobu Hashimoto helped him rewrite the whole screenplay.<br/>I'm sorry I've never seen the film before, however, some 40 years ago I read its screenplay in Thai. Therefore, it's interesting to find its c</span>
  <span id="freeText15309435841169151935" style="display:none">Reading Ryunosuke Akutakawa's "Hell Screen" is like reading Edgar Allan Poe. However, "Rashomon" here was merely the inception of the 1950 film directed by Akira Kurosawa since, according to the Translator's Note, the director used only the first two short stories (Rashomon &amp; In a Bamboo Grove) and Shinobu Hashimoto helped him rewrite the whole screenplay.<br />I'm sorry I've never seen the film before, however, some 40 years ago I read its screenplay in Thai. Therefore, it's interesting to find its complete screenplay to read and compare how the screenplay writers created further plots/stories from the two short stories. For more details, please visit this website:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rashomon_(film)">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rashomon...</a><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15309435841169151935" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2605228350" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2605228350" />
    <a title="Quiver" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9310150-quiver"><img alt="Quiver" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1489326638p2/9310150.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2605228350?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Quiver" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Quiver" href="/user/show/9310150-quiver">Quiver</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140449709" href="/book/show/13419553-rash-mon-and-seventeen-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9310150-quiver?shelf=a-english">a-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9310150-quiver?shelf=in-translation-ja-ko-zh">in-translation-ja-ko-zh</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9310150-quiver?shelf=s-short-stories">s-short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2605228350" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10176419115879105276">Kurosawa's 1950 film <em> Rashōmon </em> gave rise to the so called <em>Rashomon effect</em>: an effect that occurs when we are presented with multiple, clashing interpretations of the same event. <br/><br/>Contrary to my expectations, Akutagawa's eponymous story lends nothing to the concept, save its title, and it is another story from the same collection, <em>In a Bamboo Grove</em>, that provides the basis for the film and embodies the effect itself (intriguingly so).<br/><br/>The collection is divided into four thematically connected sect</span>
  <span id="freeText10176419115879105276" style="display:none">Kurosawa's 1950 film <em> Rashōmon </em> gave rise to the so called <em>Rashomon effect</em>: an effect that occurs when we are presented with multiple, clashing interpretations of the same event. <br /><br />Contrary to my expectations, Akutagawa's eponymous story lends nothing to the concept, save its title, and it is another story from the same collection, <em>In a Bamboo Grove</em>, that provides the basis for the film and embodies the effect itself (intriguingly so).<br /><br />The collection is divided into four thematically connected sections: A World in Decay, Under the Sword, Modern Tragicomedy, Akutagawa's Own Story. The first section contains <em>Rashōmon</em> and <em>In tha Bamboo Grove</em> and four other original, quirky tales a Western reader would easily appreciate. Of the rest, only <em>Horse Legs</em> in Modern Tragicomedy was readable and relatable enough without extraordinary effort. Some of Akutagawa's Own Story is insightful, lyrical, and potentially quite interesting from the autobiographical perspective.<br /><br /><blockquote><br />Yet even a desolate life can reveal a world of beauty when viewed through a mist of tears.<br /></blockquote> <br /><br />There is always something poignant about the stories of a writer who took his own life...</span>
  <a data-text-id="10176419115879105276" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1872696314" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7357537389346479218">I like how the stories are being told, told by a master storyteller. However, to talk about thieves, corpses, and more corpses and such morbid things doesn't appeal to me. At least, I gave it a try and came out of my literary shell :)</span>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VnFN668Gd8r1QZB0LO5Vl7z5cbKFAOx4/gsZeEaU6H+TX25ikeaOL21JNxBpMFP2LRTPlnMBMSJltwr/FM3yWA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vTwkVgjXTMCTmmgkRFj1G88IsFSd+aQx82MmgZDkO4p4EgffNje1JQuSz0ABhvN6XuUOcGv4eWto3zUGwr0hrQ==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_12218696'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12218696-otogiz-shi?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Otogizōshi: The Fairy Tale Book of Dazai Osamu<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/113561.Osamu_Dazai?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Osamu Dazai<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.94 avg rating &mdash; 395 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1945\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer18159728842500882342\">Dazai Osamu wrote The Fairy Tale Book (Otogizōshi) in the last months of the Pacific War. The traditional tales upon which Dazai\'s retellings are based are well known to every Japanese schoolchild, but this is no children\'s book. In Dazai\'s hands suc<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText18159728842500882342\" style=\"display:none\">Dazai Osamu wrote The Fairy Tale Book (Otogizōshi) in the last months of the Pacific War. The traditional tales upon which Dazai\'s retellings are based are well known to every Japanese schoolchild, but this is no children\'s book. In Dazai\'s hands such stock characters as the kindhearted Oji-san to Oba-san (&quot;Grandmother and Grandfather&quot;), the mischievous tanuki badger, the fearsome Oni ogres, the greedy old man, the &quot;tongue-cut&quot; sparrow, and of course Urashima Taro (the Japanese Rip van Winkle) become complex individuals facing difficult and nuanced moral dilemmas. The resulting stories are thought-provoking, slyly subversive, and often hilarious.\n\nIn spite of the &quot;gloom and doom&quot; atmosphere always cited in reviews of The Setting Sun and the later No Longer Human, though, Dazai\'s cutting wit and rich humor are evident in the entire body of his work. His literature depicts the human condition in painfully blunt and realistic terms, but, like life itself, is often accompanied by a smile.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"18159728842500882342\" Osamu Dazai’s The Setting Sun takes this milieu as its background to tell the story of the decline of a minor aristocratic family.\n\nThe story is told through the eyes of Kazuko, the unmarried daughter of a widowed aristocrat. Her search for self meaning in a society devoid of use for her forms the crux of Dazai’s novel. It is a sad story, and structurally is a novel very much within the confines of the Japanese take on the novel in a way reminiscent of authors such as Nobel Prize winner Yasunari Kawabata – the social interactions are peripheral and understated, nuances must be drawn, and for readers more used to Western novelistic forms this comes across as being rather wishy-washy.\n\nKazuko’s mother falls ill, and due to their financial circumstances they are forced to take a cottage in the countryside. Her brother, who became addicted to opium during the war is missing. When he returns, Kazuko attempts to form a liaison with the novelist Uehara. Among Japanese readers both young and old it has enjoyed a timeless popularity, making it, according to Donald Keene, &quot;probably the most widely read novel in modern Japan.&quot;\n\nThe setting is Japan\'s deep south, where the author himself spent some time teaching English in a boys\' school. Into this conservative world, with its social proprieties and established pecking order, breezes Botchan, down from the big city, with scant respect for either his elders or his noisy young charges; and the result is a chain of collisions large and small.\n\nMuch of the story seems to occur in summer, against the drone of cicadas, and in many ways this is a summer book light, funny, never slow-moving. Here, in a lively new translation much better suited to Western tastes than any of its forebears, Botchan\'s homespun appeal is all the more apparent, and even those who have never been near the sunlit island on which these calamitous episodes take place should find in it uninterrupted entertainment.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"2706274109387140240\" href=\"#\"
